The Scope of Sahih and Fasid: Only Worship and Contracts
Al-Ajhuri specifies the domain of sahih and fasid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the terms sahih (sound) and fasid (corrupt) do not apply to all prohibited (ḥaram) or disliked (makrūh) acts, but only to those that are acts of worship (ʿibādāt) or contracts (ʿuqūd).

Core Idea
Not every sin is evaluated as valid or invalid.

In this passage, al-Ajhuri clarifies an important nuance in legal theory: the categories of sahih and fasid are not synonymous with permissibility and prohibition. Rather, they specifically relate to the validity of acts of worship and contracts. This prevents a common misunderstanding that any ḥaram act would be considered fasid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice that it discusses two categories: ḥaram and makrūh.
2
Identify the condition for each category
The passage states that sahih and fasid apply to ḥaram acts only if they are from ʿibādāt or ʿuqūd. For makrūh, they apply only if the act itself is from those two categories.
Look for the exception clauses: 'بخلاف نحو الزنا' and 'بخلاف نحو أكل البصل'.
3
Reflect on the examples
Contrast the examples: prayer in usurped land (ʿibādah) vs. adultery (pure sin); prayer in bathhouse (ʿibādah) vs. eating onions (non-transactional).
This illustrates why sahih/fasid is not a general moral category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من العبادات والعقود كالصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة وبيع العنب لمن يتخذه خمراً، بخلاف نحو الزنا، فإنه لا يتصف بهما، وكذا لا يجريان في المكروه إلا إذا كان منهما كالصلاة في الحمام وكبيع يكون مكروهاً إن فرض ذلك، بخلاف نحو أكل البصل، فإنه لا يتصف بهما.
From acts of worship and contracts, such as prayer in usurped land and selling grapes to one who makes it into wine, unlike something like adultery, for it is not described by them [i.e., sahih and fasid]. Likewise, they do not apply in the disliked (makruh) unless they are from among them [acts of worship and contracts], such as prayer in the bathhouse and a sale that is disliked if that is assumed, unlike something like eating onions, for it is not described by them.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This gloss addresses a technical point about the categories of sahih (sound) and fasid (corrupt) within the scheme of khitab al-wad'. It states that sahih and fasid do not apply to every prohibited or disliked act. For prohibited acts (haram), they apply only to those that are themselves acts of worship or contracts, like prayer in a usurped place or a forbidden sale. Pure sins like adultery are not described as sahih or fasid because these terms are relevant to validity and invalidity in legal transactions, not to sinfulness. Similarly, for disliked acts (makruh), sahih and fasid apply only when the act is of the type that can be valid or invalid, such as prayer in a bathhouse (which may be valid or invalid depending on conditions) or a disliked sale. Acts like eating onions, which are merely disliked but not transactional, are not described by these categories.

Key Terms
الصحة
الفساد
المحرم
المكروه
العبادات
العقود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any ḥarām or makrūh act is automatically considered fāsid (corrupt).
Correction: The passage clarifies that ṣaḥīḥ and fāsid are terms of legal validity (ṣiḥḥa/buṭlān) limited to acts of worship and contracts. A sin like adultery is not evaluated as ṣaḥīḥ or fāsid because it is not a transaction—it is simply prohibited.

Checkpoint

B) Eating pork
C) Prayer in usurped land
D) Drinking wine

Key Takeaway
Ṣaḥīḥ and fāsid are legal terms of validity limited to acts of worship and contracts; not every sin or disliked act is evaluated as valid or invalid.

?
This lesson shows that even contracts like selling grapes can be evaluated as valid or invalid based on their permissibility. Consider a recent financial transaction or business deal you were involved in. Were you more concerned with its legal validity or with its moral and ethical dimensions? How does this passage challenge you to balance legal correctness with spiritual integrity? What specific step will you take this week to align a pending transaction with both?
?
The passage distinguishes between acts that are 'merely' disliked (like eating onions) and those that are disliked but still part of worship or contracts (like prayer in a bathhouse). Reflect: In your daily life, do you sometimes avoid a valid act of worship because of a minor disliked aspect, thereby missing its immense reward? Or do you take such disliked factors too lightly? Definition of Hasr and Its Two Types
Sanusi defines hasr and its two types with conditions; al-Ajhuri critiques them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of hasr (restriction) in logic and the conditions for its two main types: restriction of a universal to its particulars and restriction of a whole to its parts.

Core Idea
Hasr is defined by the relationship between the restricted and restricting, not by a single rule.

Sanusi introduces the concept of hasr (restriction) and enumerates two of its types. He provides conditions for each, but the commentary by al-Ajhuri refines these conditions, showing they actually describe the nature of the restricted and restricting, not the act of restriction itself. This lesson will clarify the definitions and highlight the logical nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: Sanusi states two types with conditions, and al-Ajhuri adds a critical note.
The commentary corrects a potential misunderstanding about the conditions.
2
Understand the first type: universal to particulars
Identify the condition: the divided entity (universal) can be predicated of each division. Example: 'word' divided into noun, verb, particle – each is a word.
3
Understand the second type: whole to parts
Identify the condition: the divided entity (whole) can be analyzed into its parts. Example: a mat analyzed into fiber and thread. Note the commentary's criticism: this condition fails for mixtures like sakanjabin.
Al-Ajhuri suggests an alternative condition for this type.
4
Grasp the commentary's critique
Al-Ajhuri points out that the given conditions are not for hasr itself but for the restricted being universal or whole. Also, the second condition is not universal because not all wholes can be physically analyzed.
This shows the importance of precise definitions in kalam.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check if you can recall the condition for the first type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: يَنْحَصِرُ فِي ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامِ اعلم أن الحصر على ثلاثة أقسام: الأولُ: حَصْرُ الكُلّي في جُزئياته، وضابطة: أن يَصِحُ الإخبارُ بالمُقْسم عن كلّ قسم من أقسامه، كما في حصر الكلمة في الاسم والفعل والحرف؛ إذ يصح أن تقول: الاسم كلمةً وهكذا. والثاني: حَصْرُ الكُلِّ في أجزائه، وضابطة أن يصح تحليل المقسم إلى أقسامه، كما في حصر الحصير في السَّمَارِ والخيط؛ إذ يصح تحليله إليهما. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري: (قوله: اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْحَضرَ عَلَى ثَلَاثَة ستعلم أنه أكثرُ من ذلك. (قوله: وَضَابِطُهُ أَنْ يَصِحٌ... إلخ) فيه: أن هذا ضابط لكون المحصور كلياً، والمحصور فيه جزئياً، لا للحصر . (قوله: وَضَابِطهُ: أَنْ يَصِحْ تَخلِيلُ ... إلخ) فيه: أن هذا ضابط كَوْنِ المحصور كلاً، والمحصور فيه أجزاء، لا للحصر. ثم إن الظاهر أن المراد الفعليُّ الخارجي، لا التحليل باللفظ والعبارة. وفيه أن هذا يَطْرِدُ؛ إذ السَّكْنَجِينُ لا يتأكَّى تحليله إلى الخَلِ والعَسَلِ؛ إذ لا يمكن تمييز أحدهما من الآخر، فلو قال: أن لا يصح الإخبارُ بالمقسم عن كل قسم؛ لاطَّرَدَ.
His saying: 'It is restricted to three divisions.' Know that restriction is of three types: The first: restriction of the universal to its particulars, and its condition is that it is valid to predicate the divided entity of each of its divisions, as in restricting the word to noun, verb, and particle; for it is valid to say: 'The noun is a word', and so on. The second: restriction of the whole to its parts, and its condition is that it is valid to analyze the divided entity into its parts, as in restricting a mat to samar and thread; for it is valid to analyze it into them. Hashiyah of al-Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'Know that restriction is of three types' — you will know that it is more than that. (His saying: 'And its condition is that it is valid... etc.') There is in it: that this condition is for the restricted being a universal and the restricting being particulars, not for restriction itself. (His saying: 'And its condition is that it is valid to analyze... etc.') There is in it: that this condition is for the restricted being a whole and the restricting being parts, not for restriction itself. Then it is apparent that what is meant is actual external analysis, not analysis by word and expression. But there is in it that this is not universal; for vinegar-honey syrup cannot be analyzed into vinegar and honey, since it is not possible to distinguish one from the other. So if he had said: 'that it is not valid to predicate the divided entity of each division,' it would be universal.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'hasr' (restriction) as used in logic and usul al-fiqh. The matn states that restriction is limited to three types, but the commentary immediately notes that there are more. The first type is restriction of a universal to its particulars (al-kulli fi juz'iyyatih), where the condition is that the divided entity (the universal) can be truly predicated of each of its divisions. For example, 'word' is a universal divided into noun, verb, and particle; you can say 'the noun is a word,' and similarly for verb and particle. The second type is restriction of a whole to its parts (al-kulli fi ajza'ih), where the condition is that the divided entity can be analyzed into its parts. For example, a mat is analyzed into samar (a plant fiber) and thread. The commentary by al-Ajhuri clarifies that these conditions are actually for the nature of the restricted and restricting, not for the act of restriction itself. He also points out that the second condition is not universally applicable because some wholes cannot be physically analyzed, like vinegar-honey syrup (sakanjabin) which is a mixture that cannot be separated into its components. The commentary suggests an alternative formulation: 'that it is not valid to predicate the divided entity of each division' would be a universal condition for the second type. The passage thus teaches the definitions and conditions for two of the three types of hasr, with a critical analysis of the conditions.

Key Terms
حصر
كلّي
جزئي
أجزاء
ضابط
تحليل
مقسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the conditions given (predicability for universal/particular, analyzability for whole/part) are conditions for the act of hasr itself.
Correction: Al-Ajhuri clarifies that these conditions are actually for the nature of the restricted and restricting (i.e., that the restricted is a universal or whole, and the restricting are particulars or parts), not for hasr as an operation.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for the first type of hasr (restriction of the universal to its particulars)?
A) It is valid to analyze the divided entity into its parts
B) It is valid to predicate the divided entity of each division
C) The divided entity must be a whole
D) The restriction must be physical

Key Takeaway
Hasr (restriction) is defined by the logical relationship between the restricted and restricting, and the conditions given in the matn actually describe that relationship, not the act of restriction itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the phrase 'the conditions of faith,' have you ever stopped to examine whether those conditions truly define faith or merely describe aspects of it? How will you verify it this week?

Think of one such rule in your worship or dealings with others. What concrete step can you take today to test its universality and adjust if needed?

Or do you assume you already know? Commit now to pick one ayah this week and study its logical implications for your belief—write down what you find.

Distinguishing the Two Meanings of Wujub
Al-Bajuri distinguishes the kalam meaning of wujub from its common juristic meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the crucial terminological distinction between the kalam meaning of wujub (necessary existence) and the fiqh meaning (moral obligation) and why confusing them leads to errors.

Core Idea
Wujub in kalam means necessity, not obligation—confusing them undermines theology.

Knowing modal terms in kalam is essential, but the same word can have a different meaning in fiqh. This passage clarifies that when wujub is used absolutely in tawhid, it refers to necessary existence, except when applied to human responsibility. Let's read Al-Bajuri's warning carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
The passage is short but dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two meanings
The passage tells us that 'wujub' has two distinct meanings: one used in kalam (necessary existence) and another in fiqh (moral obligation, reward/punishment). Find how the author describes each.
The 'well-known meaning' refers to the juristic sense.
3
Note the exception in usage
The author says that when the word is used in phrases like 'it is obligatory upon the responsible person to know...' it takes the fiqh meaning, not the kalam meaning.
This exception prevents confusion in statements about human duties.
4
Understand the warning
Al-Bajuri warns against mixing the two meanings, which leads to meaningless statements. Be careful to note the context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الوجوب بذلك المعنى هو المراد في علم التوحيد متى أُطلِق، إلا في نحو قولهم: يجب على كلّ مكلَّف أن يَعْرِفَ... إلخ، فهو فيه بالمعنى المشهور، وهو كَوْنُ الشيء بحيث يُثَابُ على فعله ويعاقب على تركه، ففَرْقٌ بين أن يقال: الله كذا وأن يقال: يجب على المكلف كذا فاخرض على هذا الفرق، ولا تكُنْ من اشتبه عليه الأمرُ فقال ما لا مُحَصَّلَ له.
And know that wujub in that meaning [the one previously defined] is what is intended in the science of tawhid whenever it is used absolutely, except in such statements as 'It is obligatory (yajibu) upon every legally responsible person to know...' etc., for there it is in the well-known meaning [of obligation], which is that a thing is such that one is rewarded for doing it and punished for abandoning it. So there is a difference between saying 'Allah is such-and-such' and saying 'It is obligatory upon the responsible person such-and-such'. So be careful to note this difference, and do not be one for whom the matter is confused and who says something with no substance.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a crucial terminological clarification. In kalam, the term 'wujub' when used absolutely refers to necessary existence (i.e., the modal status of being necessary). However, in other contexts, such as the phrase 'it is obligatory upon every responsible person to know...', the word is used in its well-known Shar'i meaning of moral obligation, where doing the act brings reward and abandoning it brings punishment. The author warns the student to distinguish carefully between these two usages—one is a technical term in tawhid, the other is a legal term. Confusing them leads to meaningless statements. The admonition 'do not be one for whom the matter is confused' underscores the importance of this point.

Key Terms
الوجوب
علم التوحيد
المعنى المشهور
مكلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Students think wujub always means moral obligation in Islamic texts.
Correction: In kalam, wujub (when used absolutely) means necessary existence, not obligation. Only when applied to human actions does it take the fiqh meaning of obligation.

Checkpoint

A) Moral obligation that brings reward
B) Necessary existence (non-acceptance of non-existence)
C) Legal duty
D) Acceptance of both existence and non-existence

Key Takeaway
The term wujub has two distinct meanings: necessary existence in kalam and moral obligation in fiqh; mixing them leads to confusion.

Predication of Agreement and Derivation
Al-Bajuri defines two types of predication with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between direct (agreement) predication and derivative predication, and why the latter requires interpretation (ta’wīl).

Core Idea
Not all predications are literal; some need interpretation to avoid confusion.

Al-Bajuri introduces two fundamental types of predication used in Islamic logic and theology. Understanding this distinction helps avoid misinterpretation when attributes are ascribed to subjects. In this lesson, we will examine the definitions and examples, and note a common scribal error that highlights the importance of context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the Arabic text says both types do not require ta'wil, but the context suggests otherwise.
2
Identify the two types and their definitions
The passage presents two types: predication of agreement (haml al-muwata'a) and predication of derivation (haml al-ishtiqaq). Find how each is defined and what examples are given.
Focus on the examples: 'al-Imam al-Shafi'i is knowledge' and 'cotton is whiteness'.
3
Note the scribal error and correct interpretation
The text says both do not require interpretation, but standard logic holds that derivative predication does require ta'wil. Consider why this correction is necessary based on the examples.
This error is a common point of confusion; the intended meaning is that only agreement predication is literal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحَمْلُ المواطأة: هو ما لا يحتاج لتأويل كما ذكره، ويقابِلُهُ: حَمْلُ الاشتقاق: وهو ما لا يحتاجُ لذلك، كقولك: الإمام الشافعي عِلْمٌ ، أي : ذُو عِلْمٍ أو عالم، والقُطنُ بياضُ أي: ذو بياض أو أبيضُ.
And predication of agreement (ḥaml al-muwāṭa’a) is that which does not require interpretation (ta’wīl) as he mentioned, and its counterpart is predication of derivation (ḥaml al-ishtiqāq): and it does not require that, like your saying: 'al-Imām al-Shāfiʿī is knowledge (ʿilm)', meaning 'possessor of knowledge (dhū ʿilm)' or 'knowledgeable (ʿālim)', and 'cotton is whiteness (bayāḍ)', meaning 'possessor of whiteness (dhū bayāḍ)' or 'white (abyaḍ)'.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage defines two types of predication (ḥaml) used in Arabic logic and grammar. The first is ḥaml al-muwāṭa’a (predication of agreement), which is a direct predication where the predicate is said to be identical with the subject in essence, requiring no interpretation. For example, 'Zayd is a human' – human and Zayd are essentially the same. The second is ḥaml al-ishtiqāq (predication of derivation), which requires interpretation because the predicate is not identical to the subject but is derived from it. The text gives examples: saying 'al-Imām al-Shāfiʿī is knowledge' would be understood to mean 'he is knowledgeable (ʿālim)' or 'possessor of knowledge'. Similarly, 'cotton is whiteness' means 'it is white' or 'possessor of whiteness'. In derivative predication, the predicate is not the same as the subject; it is an attribute inhering in it, so it needs interpretation (ta’wīl) to avoid confusion. The definition in the text seems to have a typo: it says both types 'do not require interpretation', but the context clearly shows that derivative predication does require interpretation. The intended meaning is that predication of agreement does not require interpretation, while predication of derivation does.

Key Terms
حمل المواطأة
حمل الاشتقاق
تأويل
علم
بياض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Both types of predication are literal and require no interpretation.
Correction: Derivative predication (ḥaml al-ishtiqāq) requires interpretation (ta’wīl) because the predicate is not identical to the subject; it indicates an attribute.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the characteristic of ḥaml al-ishtiqāq (predication of derivation)?
A) It does not require interpretation.
B) It requires interpretation (ta’wīl) to explain the meaning.
C) It is used only for God's attributes.
D) It means the predicate is identical to the subject.

Key Takeaway
Derivative predication requires interpretation (ta’wīl) to correctly understand the relationship between subject and predicate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe someone as 'knowledgeable' or 'wise', do you ever consider whether your words are literal predications or require interpretation? How does this distinction affect your understanding of human attributes?
?
The source says 'cotton is whiteness' is not literal but means 'possessor of whiteness'. Think of a time you mistook a metaphorical statement for a literal one. Did that lead to confusion? How can this lesson help you avoid that in matters of faith?
?
In your own beliefs about Allah's attributes, are there any you have understood as literal predications when they might actually be derivative? How would a proper ta’wīl change your understanding? Commit to re-examining one attribute this week.

6 min
The Derivation of 'Mustahil': Does It Imply a Request?
Al-Bajuri and al-Ajhuri debate the derivation of 'mustahil' and the request claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sīn and tā' in 'mustahil' do not indicate a request, and how Arabic morphology affects theological terminology.

Core Idea
Not every 'mustaf'il' pattern implies a request; context and transitivity matter.

This lesson unpacks a linguistic debate over the derivation of 'mustahil' (the impossible). Some claimed the sīn and tā' prefix signals a request, but scholars showed why that interpretation is problematic. Understanding this helps us appreciate the precision of kalam terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once to get an overall sense, then read the translation. Note that there are two separate annotations: one by al-Bajuri, then by al-Ajhuri.
Pay attention to the different voices and how they respond to each other.
2
Identify the two opposing views
First, find the claim that sīn and tā' indicate request. Then find the weakening argument. Notice that al-Bajuri reports the claim and its weakening, while al-Ajhuri expands on the linguistic details.
The claim is that 'mustahil' means the responsible person is asked to consider it impossible.
3
Understand the linguistic objection
Focus on the objection that 'istahāla' is not transitive like 'istaghfara'. This is a key morphological argument. Reflect on why this refutes the request interpretation.
Transitivity is crucial: request verbs in Arabic are transitive, but 'istahāla' is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية البيجوري على السنوسية (قوله: (وَالْمُسْتَحِيلُ) قيل: السينُ والتاء فيه للطلب بمعنى: أنه طَلَب من المكلَّف أن يُحِيلَهُ - أي يعتقد أنه محال .. وضُعِفَ: بأن هذا اسم لنحو الشريك بقطع النظر عن الطلب، وهذا يُوهِمُ أنه منظورٌ للطلب في هذه التسمية، وليس كذلك. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ طُلِبَ مِنَ الْمَكَلَّفِ أَنْ يُحِيلَهُ أي طَلَبَ الله من المكلف أن يجعله على ما يتبادَرُ منه وفيه أن (المستحيل) على جعل السين والتاء للطلب معناه: طالب الإحالة؛ لأن (مستحيلاً) اسم فاعل من: أَحَالَ زيدتِ السينُ والتاء فيه للدلالة على الطلب، فيكون طالب الإحالة هو، نحو الشريك على سبيل المبالغة في الإحالة، لا الله، إلا أن يقال: فيه من التكلف. إن قوله: (بمعنى أنه طلب... إلخ) بيان للمقصود من اللفظ لا للمعنى الوضعي، ولا يخفى ما وردَّ بعضُ مشايخنا كَوْنَهما للطلب بأن استحال ليس متعدياً كاستغفرتُ الله .واستقدرتُه ورُدَّ كونُهما للتعدي بما ذكر أيضاً. (قوله: وَضُعَفَ بِأَنَّ هَذَا... إلخ) قد علمت تضعيفه بوجه آخر خلافاً لما يوهِمُهُ كلامه. فتدبر.
Annotation of al-Bajuri on al-Sanussiyya: (His saying: 'and the impossible (al-mustahil)') it was said: the sīn and tā' in it are for request, meaning that the responsible person is asked to consider it impossible - i.e., to believe it is impossible. And it was weakened: because this is a name for something like the partner (al-sharik) regardless of the request, and this suggests that the request is considered in this naming, but it is not so. Annotation of al-Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'meaning that he is asked to consider it impossible') i.e., God asks the responsible person to make it as it is understood from it. And in it there is that (al-mustahil) on the assumption that sīn and tā' are for request means: the one who seeks declaring impossible, because 'mustahil' is the active participle from 'ahāla' with sīn and tā' added to indicate request, so it becomes the one who seeks declaring impossibility, like 'al-sharik' by way of exaggeration in declaring impossibility, not God, unless it is said: there is affectation in that. Indeed his saying 'meaning that he is asked... etc.' is an explanation of the intended meaning of the word, not the lexical meaning. And it is not hidden what: and some of our masters refuted that they (sīn and tā') are for request by saying that 'istahāla' is not transitive like 'istaghfartu Allah' and 'istaqdartuhu', and the claim that they are for transitivity was refuted by what was mentioned. (His saying: 'and it was weakened by that this... etc.') you have known its weakening in another way contrary to what his statement suggests. So contemplate.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage examines the Arabic derivation of 'mustahil' (impossible). Al-Bajuri reports a claim that the sīn and tā' at the beginning of 'mustahil' indicate a request (talab), implying that the word means 'that which the responsible person is asked to consider impossible'. This claim is weakened because words on the pattern 'mustaf'il' are names for certain concepts (like 'al-sharik' meaning the partner) without necessarily implying a request. If we force the request meaning, it would suggest that the request is considered in the naming, but that is not the case. Al-Ajhuri then elaborates that according to the request interpretation, 'mustahil' would be an active participle meaning 'the one who seeks to declare impossibility', referring to God's command to believers, but this is forced. He notes that some scholars objected that the verb 'istahāla' is not transitive like 'istaghfara' (to seek forgiveness) and 'istaqdara' (to seek capacity), so the sīn and tā' cannot be for transitivity or request in this pattern. The discussion highlights the importance of Arabic morphology in theological terms and cautions against over-interpreting morphological patterns.

Key Terms
المستحيل
السين والتاء
الطلب
استحال
الإحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sīn and tā' in 'mustahil' necessarily indicate a request, so the word means 'that which is asked to be considered impossible'.
Correction: The pattern 'mustaf'il' does not always imply request; it is often just a name. Moreover, the verb 'istahāla' is not transitive, so the request interpretation is morphologically invalid.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument against interpreting 'mustahil' as derived from a request form?
A) The word is not an active participle
B) The verb 'istahāla' is not transitive like 'istaghfara'
C) The sīn and tā' are always for emphasis
D) The word refers only to God's actions

Key Takeaway
The sīn and tā' in 'mustahil' are not for request; the word is simply a name for the impossible, grounded in Arabic morphological patterns.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a theological term like 'mustahil' without questioning its linguistic roots? How might that casual acceptance affect the precision of your understanding of Allah's attributes? Commit today to investigating the morphology of at least one Islamic term you use regularly.
?
When you hear scholars debating subtle linguistic points, do you dismiss it as nitpicking or recognize it as protecting the integrity of divine speech? Will you from now on pay closer attention to such debates, even if they seem difficult, knowing they safeguard your 'aqeedah?

The Mutawa'a Interpretation of 'Mustahil'
Presentation and critique of the mutawa'a interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the interpretation that 'mustahil' derives as the passive/reflexive (mutawa'a) of 'ahala', and the critique that this implies an accidental attribute.

Core Idea
'Mustahil' as mutawa'a suggests the attribute is accidental, not essential.

The analysis begins with lexicographical evidence that 'istaf'ala' often functions as a mutawa'a of 'af'ala'. Commentators explore whether 'mustahil' fits this pattern, leading to a critique about the nature of impossibility as a description that could be accidentally acquired.

Guided Path
1
Read the lexicographical foundation
Review the citations from 'al-Tashil' and 'al-Qamus' establishing that 'istaf'ala' can be mutawa'a and that 'muhal' means turned away.
Focus on the root meaning of tahawwul (turning).
2
Understand the mutawa'a reading
See how the meaning is developed: 'ahaltuhu fastahala' implies that one considers something impossible and it becomes impossible through that consideration.
Note the two possible subjects: human or God.
3
Grasp the critique
The critique says this reading falsely implies that the description of impossibility is accidental, arising from an external influence, whereas impossibility is an essential attribute.
Think about why this matters for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَاخْتَارَ بَعْضُهُمْ... إلخ) نصَّ في "التسهيل" على أن استفعل يكون مطاوعاً لأفعل، وفي "القاموس" : المُحَالُ من الكلام - بالضم - : ما عُدِلَ عن وجهه كالمستحيل اهـ. وقد تبين منه: أن الاستحالة في الأصل بمعنى: التقلب والانحراف، من: التحول، فمعنى أحاله حَرَفَهُ، فاستحال انْحَرَفَ. والمعنى على المطاوَعَةِ: أَحَلْتُهُ فاستحال، أي: اعْتَقَدْتُ مُحَالِيَّتَهُ فقَبِلَ ذلك الاعتقاد، وضع تعلقه به لكونه محالاً، أو المعنى: أحاله الله فاستحال. والمراد بإحالة الله : دلالته بكلامه القديم على أنه محال. وعليه يكون (مستحيل) مأخوذاً من: اسْتَحَالَ مطاوع: أحال، يقال: أَحَلْتُهُ فاستحال. كذا نقله اليُوسِيُّ عن بعض مشايخه. ثم قال: وهو الظاهر اهـ. ونظر فيه: بأن المطاوَعَةَ تُوهِمُ أن هذا وَصْفٌ طَرَاً بتأثير الغير، وليس كذلك. ولا يصح أن يكونا للصيرورة؛ لأنها تقتضي أنه لم يكن مُحَالاً ثم صار، وليس كذلك. واستظهر بعض المحققين أنهما زائدتان فيكون المستحيل بمعنى: المُحال. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري :(قوله: يُقَالُ أَحَلْتُهُ لعل المعنى: أتيت بعبارة تدل على كونه محالاً بتلك العبارة. هذا ما أمكن في فهم هذه العبارة. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (قوله: وَنُظِرَ فِيهِ... إلخ) قد يقال : لا عِبْرَةَ بِالإيهام المدفوع بالقرينة، على أن الإيهام لازم على الزيادة؛ إذ العبرة في الإيهام بذات اللفظ والمفهوم من اللفظ المطاوعة لا الزيادة. (قوله: وَلَا يَصِحُ أَنْ يَكُونَا لِلصَّيْرُورَة لِأَنَّهَا تَقْتَضِي... إلخ) فيه: أن الصيرورة لا تقتضي ذلك، بل تقتضي صيرورتَهُ مُحِيلاً لما علمت أن مستحيلاً اسم فاعل من: أَحَالَ، وهو لا معنى له إلا أن يقال: المراد : صيرورتُهُ مُحِيلاً لنفسه على سبيل المبالغة نظير ما تقدم، ويلزم من ذلك: صيرورته مُحَالاً، فقد اعتبر المحشي اللازم. وقد يقال: إن معنى: مستحيل على الصيرورة أنه صار قائماً به الإحالةُ، بمعنى: الكَوْنِ مُحَالاً، فلا حاجة لما تقدم. (قوله: وَاسْتَظْهَرَ بَعْضُ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ... إلخ) ظاهرُهُ: اختيار هذا، مع أنه يَرِدُ عليه أن مستحيلاً اسم فاعل من: أَحَالَ فيقتضي أن الشريكَ مُحِيلٌ مع أنه محال، فيحتاج لتكلف الجواب عن ذلك: بأن نحو الشريك مُحِيلٌ لنفسه مبالغةً، فيكون المحيل هو المُحَالُ. ولا يخفى بُعْدُهُ. وقد يقال: إن مادة الاستحالة المزيد فيها الحرفان معناها غير مادة الإحالة، فالاستحالة: هي امتناع الوجود، بخلاف الإحالة.
(His statement: And some of them chose that it is for mutawa'a... etc.) He stated in 'al-Tashil' that 'istaf'ala' is a mutawa' (passive/reflexive) of 'af'ala', and in 'al-Qamus': al-muhal from speech - with damm - is that which is turned away from its proper course, like al-mustahil. And from this it becomes clear that istihala originally means turning and deviating, from tahawwul (transformation). So the meaning of ahala is he turned it, so istahala means it turned. And the meaning on the interpretation of mutawa'a is: I ahaltuhu (I considered it impossible) so it istahala (accepted that), i.e., I believed in its impossibility so it accepted that belief, and its relation to it is established because it is impossible, or the meaning: God ahala it so it istahala. And what is meant by God's ihala is His indication through His eternal speech that it is impossible. Accordingly, (mustahil) is derived from istahala, the mutawa' of ahala; it is said: ahaltuhu fastahala. Thus al-Yusi transmitted it from some of his shaykhs. Then he said: And it is apparent. And it was criticized: that al-mutawa'a implies that this is an accidental description by the influence of another, and it is not so. And it is not correct that they are for sayrura (becoming), because it requires that it was not impossible and then became, and it is not so. And some investigators deemed that they are redundant (za'idatan), so al-mustahil means al-muhal. Gloss by al-Allama Ahmad al-Ujhuri: (His statement: It is said ahaltuhu) perhaps the meaning: I produced an expression indicating that it is impossible by that expression. This is what was possible in understanding this phrase. Taqrirat by al-Allama Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His statement: And it was criticized... etc.) It may be said: No heed is paid to the false implication (al-iham) that is repelled by context, especially since the false implication is necessary on the interpretation of redundancy; for the consideration in false implication is the literal wording and the meaning derived from it: the wording implies mutawa'a, not redundancy. (His statement: And it is not correct that they are for sayrura because it requires... etc.) In it: That sayrura does not require that, but rather it requires his becoming muhilan (one who renders impossible), since you know that mustahil is an active participle from ahala, and that has no meaning unless it is said: what is meant is his making himself impossible by way of hyperbole, analogous to what preceded, and from that follows his becoming muhalan (impossible), so the commentator (al-muhashshi) considered the necessary consequence. And it may be said: The meaning of mustahil on the interpretation of sayrura is that he became characterized by ihala (rendering impossible), meaning being impossible, so there is no need for what preceded. (His statement: And some investigators deemed that they are redundant... etc.) Its apparent meaning is the choice of this, although it is objectionable because mustahil is an active participle from ahala, which would imply that the partner (in divinity) is muhil while it is impossible, so it requires a contrived answer: that something like the partner makes itself impossible hyperbolically, so the muhil is the muhal. And its remoteness is not hidden. And it may be said: The root of istihala with the increased two letters has a meaning different from the root of ihala, for istihala is the impossibility of existence, unlike ihala.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a classification and evaluation of multiple linguistic interpretations of the word 'mustahil' (impossible). It begins by presenting the view of some scholars that 'istaf'ala' is a mutawa'a (passive/reflexive form) of 'af'ala', citing lexicons. The meaning is then that a person considers something impossible and it becomes impossible. A criticism follows: this interpretation implies the description is accidental, not essential. Next, the view that it is for sayrura (becoming) is rejected because it would imply a temporal change from possibility to impossibility. Some investigators then propose that the extra letters are redundant, making 'mustahil' simply synonymous with 'muhal'. Al-Ajhuri offers a gloss on the expression 'ahaltuhu'. Al-Anbabi provides detailed critiques: he argues that the false implication of mutawa'a is negated by context, and that the redundancy view also carries false implication. He then analyzes the sayrura interpretation, suggesting it could mean 'becoming one who renders impossible' hyperbolically, leading to the necessary consequence of being impossible. Finally, he notes an objection to the redundancy view: it would imply that a false deity is an 'agent of rendering impossible', which is absurd. He concludes by noting a distinction between the root of istihala and that of ihala, where istihala means absolute impossibility of existence.

Key Terms
استفعل
مطاوع
إحالة
استحالة
محال
مستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutawa'a interpretation is the only valid one and faces no objections.
Correction: The text shows it was criticized for implying an accidental attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the critique of the mutawa'a interpretation of 'mustahil'?
A) It violates Arabic grammar rules.
B) It implies the attribute of impossibility is accidental.
C) It cannot be applied to divine attributes.
D) It contradicts the Quranic usage.

Key Takeaway
The mutawa'a interpretation of 'mustahil' is criticized because it suggests impossibility is an accidental attribute, which conflicts with its essential nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a difficult command of Allah as 'impossible' for you, without considering that your judgment might be based on your own limitations? How does the warning against making impossibility dependent on human perception challenge your attitude toward divine obligations?
?
The mutawa'a interpretation suggests that something becomes impossible only when it is considered so. In your life, do you treat the teachings of Islam as essential truths independent of your acceptance, or do you subtly let your own reasoning decide what is 'possible' and 'impossible' for you to follow?
?
If you realized that your sense of what is 'impossible' for God to require of you is often just a reflection of your own weakness, what would you need to change? Name one practical step you can take today to submit to a command you have been avoiding.

The Sayrura Interpretation of 'Mustahil'
Rejection of the sayrura interpretation with a counter-analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the interpretation of 'mustahil' as derived from sayrura (becoming) is rejected because it implies a temporal change.

Core Idea
Sayrura would imply impossibility arises from a prior state of possibility.

After rejecting the mutawa'a interpretation, the text considers whether the 'istaf'ala' form could indicate sayrura (becoming). This reading would mean that something becomes impossible after not being impossible, which contradicts the timeless nature of impossibility in theology.

Guided Path
1
Recall the sayrura function of 'istaf'ala'
In Arabic, 'istaf'ala' can indicate entering into a state (sayrura). Consider the implications for 'mustahil'.
Example: 'istahwala' means 'became ugly'.
2
Read the rejection
The passage argues that sayrura would require that the thing was not impossible and then became impossible, which is not the case for eternal truths.
Focus on the phrase 'taqtadi annahu lam yakun muhalan thumma sar'.
3
Consider the counter-gloss by al-Anbabi
Al-Anbabi offers a nuanced interpretation: sayrura could mean 'became an agent of rendering impossible' hyperbolically, leading to the state of being impossible.
This is a defensive move to rescue the sayrura reading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَاخْتَارَ بَعْضُهُمْ... إلخ) نصَّ في "التسهيل" على أن استفعل يكون مطاوعاً لأفعل، وفي "القاموس" : المُحَالُ من الكلام - بالضم - : ما عُدِلَ عن وجهه كالمستحيل اهـ. وقد تبين منه: أن الاستحالة في الأصل بمعنى: التقلب والانحراف، من: التحول، فمعنى أحاله حَرَفَهُ، فاستحال انْحَرَفَ. والمعنى على المطاوَعَةِ: أَحَلْتُهُ فاستحال، أي: اعْتَقَدْتُ مُحَالِيَّتَهُ فقَبِلَ ذلك الاعتقاد، وضع تعلقه به لكونه محالاً، أو المعنى: أحاله الله فاستحال. والمراد بإحالة الله : دلالته بكلامه القديم على أنه محال. وعليه يكون (مستحيل) مأخوذاً من: اسْتَحَالَ مطاوع: أحال، يقال: أَحَلْتُهُ فاستحال. كذا نقله اليُوسِيُّ عن بعض مشايخه. ثم قال: وهو الظاهر اهـ. ونظر فيه: بأن المطاوَعَةَ تُوهِمُ أن هذا وَصْفٌ طَرَاً بتأثير الغير، وليس كذلك. ولا يصح أن يكونا للصيرورة؛ لأنها تقتضي أنه لم يكن مُحَالاً ثم صار، وليس كذلك. واستظهر بعض المحققين أنهما زائدتان فيكون المستحيل بمعنى: المُحال. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري :(قوله: يُقَالُ أَحَلْتُهُ لعل المعنى: أتيت بعبارة تدل على كونه محالاً بتلك العبارة. هذا ما أمكن في فهم هذه العبارة. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (قوله: وَنُظِرَ فِيهِ... إلخ) قد يقال : لا عِبْرَةَ بِالإيهام المدفوع بالقرينة، على أن الإيهام لازم على الزيادة؛ إذ العبرة في الإيهام بذات اللفظ والمفهوم من اللفظ المطاوعة لا الزيادة. (قوله: وَلَا يَصِحُ أَنْ يَكُونَا لِلصَّيْرُورَة لِأَنَّهَا تَقْتَضِي... إلخ) فيه: أن الصيرورة لا تقتضي ذلك، بل تقتضي صيرورتَهُ مُحِيلاً لما علمت أن مستحيلاً اسم فاعل من: أَحَالَ، وهو لا معنى له إلا أن يقال: المراد : صيرورتُهُ مُحِيلاً لنفسه على سبيل المبالغة نظير ما تقدم، ويلزم من ذلك: صيرورته مُحَالاً، فقد اعتبر المحشي اللازم. وقد يقال: إن معنى: مستحيل على الصيرورة أنه صار قائماً به الإحالةُ، بمعنى: الكَوْنِ مُحَالاً، فلا حاجة لما تقدم. (قوله: وَاسْتَظْهَرَ بَعْضُ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ... إلخ) ظاهرُهُ: اختيار هذا، مع أنه يَرِدُ عليه أن مستحيلاً اسم فاعل من: أَحَالَ فيقتضي أن الشريكَ مُحِيلٌ مع أنه محال، فيحتاج لتكلف الجواب عن ذلك: بأن نحو الشريك مُحِيلٌ لنفسه مبالغةً، فيكون المحيل هو المُحَالُ. ولا يخفى بُعْدُهُ. وقد يقال: إن مادة الاستحالة المزيد فيها الحرفان معناها غير مادة الإحالة، فالاستحالة: هي امتناع الوجود، بخلاف الإحالة.
(His statement: And some of them chose that it is for mutawa'a... etc.) He stated in 'al-Tashil' that 'istaf'ala' is a mutawa' (passive/reflexive) of 'af'ala', and in 'al-Qamus': al-muhal from speech - with damm - is that which is turned away from its proper course, like al-mustahil. And from this it becomes clear that istihala originally means turning and deviating, from tahawwul (transformation). So the meaning of ahala is he turned it, so istahala means it turned. And the meaning on the interpretation of mutawa'a is: I ahaltuhu (I considered it impossible) so it istahala (accepted that), i.e., I believed in its impossibility so it accepted that belief, and its relation to it is established because it is impossible, or the meaning: God ahala it so it istahala. And what is meant by God's ihala is His indication through His eternal speech that it is impossible. Accordingly, (mustahil) is derived from istahala, the mutawa' of ahala; it is said: ahaltuhu fastahala. Thus al-Yusi transmitted it from some of his shaykhs. Then he said: And it is apparent. And it was criticized: that al-mutawa'a implies that this is an accidental description by the influence of another, and it is not so. And it is not correct that they are for sayrura (becoming), because it requires that it was not impossible and then became, and it is not so. And some investigators deemed that they are redundant (za'idatan), so al-mustahil means al-muhal. Gloss by al-Allama Ahmad al-Ujhuri: (His statement: It is said ahaltuhu) perhaps the meaning: I produced an expression indicating that it is impossible by that expression. This is what was possible in understanding this phrase. Taqrirat by al-Allama Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His statement: And it was criticized... etc.) It may be said: No heed is paid to the false implication (al-iham) that is repelled by context, especially since the false implication is necessary on the interpretation of redundancy; for the consideration in false implication is the literal wording and the meaning derived from it: the wording implies mutawa'a, not redundancy. (His statement: And it is not correct that they are for sayrura because it requires... etc.) In it: That sayrura does not require that, but rather it requires his becoming muhilan (one who renders impossible), since you know that mustahil is an active participle from ahala, and that has no meaning unless it is said: what is meant is his making himself impossible by way of hyperbole, analogous to what preceded, and from that follows his becoming muhalan (impossible), so the commentator (al-muhashshi) considered the necessary consequence. And it may be said: The meaning of mustahil on the interpretation of sayrura is that he became characterized by ihala (rendering impossible), meaning being impossible, so there is no need for what preceded. (His statement: And some investigators deemed that they are redundant... etc.) Its apparent meaning is the choice of this, although it is objectionable because mustahil is an active participle from ahala, which would imply that the partner (in divinity) is muhil while it is impossible, so it requires a contrived answer: that something like the partner makes itself impossible hyperbolically, so the muhil is the muhal. And its remoteness is not hidden. And it may be said: The root of istihala with the increased two letters has a meaning different from the root of ihala, for istihala is the impossibility of existence, unlike ihala.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a classification and evaluation of multiple linguistic interpretations of the word 'mustahil' (impossible). It begins by presenting the view of some scholars that 'istaf'ala' is a mutawa'a (passive/reflexive form) of 'af'ala', citing lexicons. The meaning is then that a person considers something impossible and it becomes impossible. A criticism follows: this interpretation implies the description is accidental, not essential. Next, the view that it is for sayrura (becoming) is rejected because it would imply a temporal change from possibility to impossibility. Some investigators then propose that the extra letters are redundant, making 'mustahil' simply synonymous with 'muhal'. Al-Ajhuri offers a gloss on the expression 'ahaltuhu'. Al-Anbabi provides detailed critiques: he argues that the false implication of mutawa'a is negated by context, and that the redundancy view also carries false implication. He then analyzes the sayrura interpretation, suggesting it could mean 'becoming one who renders impossible' hyperbolically, leading to the necessary consequence of being impossible. Finally, he notes an objection to the redundancy view: it would imply that a false deity is an 'agent of rendering impossible', which is absurd. He concludes by noting a distinction between the root of istihala and that of ihala, where istihala means absolute impossibility of existence.

Key Terms
صيرورة
مطاوع
زائدتان
محال
مستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sayrura interpretation is completely invalid and has no scholarly support.
Correction: Al-Anbabi provides a nuanced defense, showing it is not absolutely rejected but requires reinterpretation.

Checkpoint
Why is the sayrura interpretation of 'mustahil' initially rejected?
A) It contradicts the Quranic usage of the word.
B) It implies a temporal change from non-impossible to impossible.
C) It requires believing that God changes.
D) It makes the letters redundant.

Key Takeaway
The sayrura interpretation is rejected because it would imply impossibility is temporally acquired, but al-Anbabi offers a hyperbolic reinterpretation to preserve its possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the term 'mustahil' in your own life—something you believe is impossible for you to achieve—do you view that impossibility as a fixed, eternal truth or as a temporary state that could change? How might that belief either empower or limit you?
?
Al-Anbabi suggests that the word 'mustahil' can be interpreted hyperbolically, implying a state of being impossible. When you describe yourself as 'unable' to do something, are you using a hyperbolic claim about a fixed state or a description of a current limitation? How would your actions change if you saw it as the latter?
?
The lesson rejects the sayrura interpretation because it would imply that impossibility comes into being after being possible. In your own spiritual life, do you treat God's commands as eternally impossible for you to fulfill, or do you allow for the possibility of change? What is one command you've dismissed as impossible that you need to reconsider?

The Redundancy Interpretation of 'Mustahil'
Presentation and critique of the redundancy interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the interpretation that the extra letters in 'mustahil' are redundant (za'idatan), making it synonymous with 'muhal', and the objections to this view.

Core Idea
If 'mustahil' is just 'muhal', it avoids temporal implications but creates grammatical and theological puzzles.

The final position examined is that the 'istaf'ala' form has redundant letters, so 'mustahil' means exactly the same as 'muhal'. This avoids the accidental and temporal problems of the other views, but introduces new difficulties: the active participle form would imply that the impossible thing is an 'agent' of rendering impossible, which is absurd for attributes of God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the redundancy claim
Some investigators deemed the 'alif' and 'ta' as redundant, so 'mustahil' = 'muhal'. Read the passage where this view is introduced.
Look for the phrase 'za'idatan fayakoonu al-mustahil bi ma'na al-muhal'.
2
Note the grammatical objection
The objection is that 'mustahil' is an active participle from 'ahala', which would imply that the impossible thing itself 'does' the rendering impossible, which is contradictory.
Especially problematic for divine attributes: would a false deity be 'muhil'?
3
Consider the proposed solutions
Al-Anbabi offers a hyperbolic interpretation (agent makes itself impossible) or suggests the root meanings differ between istihala and ihala.
The latter solution makes 'mustahil' a distinct term meaning 'absolute impossibility'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَاخْتَارَ بَعْضُهُمْ... إلخ) نصَّ في "التسهيل" على أن استفعل يكون مطاوعاً لأفعل، وفي "القاموس" : المُحَالُ من الكلام - بالضم - : ما عُدِلَ عن وجهه كالمستحيل اهـ. وقد تبين منه: أن الاستحالة في الأصل بمعنى: التقلب والانحراف، من: التحول، فمعنى أحاله حَرَفَهُ، فاستحال انْحَرَفَ. والمعنى على المطاوَعَةِ: أَحَلْتُهُ فاستحال، أي: اعْتَقَدْتُ مُحَالِيَّتَهُ فقَبِلَ ذلك الاعتقاد، وضع تعلقه به لكونه محالاً، أو المعنى: أحاله الله فاستحال. والمراد بإحالة الله : دلالته بكلامه القديم على أنه محال. وعليه يكون (مستحيل) مأخوذاً من: اسْتَحَالَ مطاوع: أحال، يقال: أَحَلْتُهُ فاستحال. كذا نقله اليُوسِيُّ عن بعض مشايخه. ثم قال: وهو الظاهر اهـ. ونظر فيه: بأن المطاوَعَةَ تُوهِمُ أن هذا وَصْفٌ طَرَاً بتأثير الغير، وليس كذلك. ولا يصح أن يكونا للصيرورة؛ لأنها تقتضي أنه لم يكن مُحَالاً ثم صار، وليس كذلك. واستظهر بعض المحققين أنهما زائدتان فيكون المستحيل بمعنى: المُحال. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري :(قوله: يُقَالُ أَحَلْتُهُ لعل المعنى: أتيت بعبارة تدل على كونه محالاً بتلك العبارة. هذا ما أمكن في فهم هذه العبارة. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (قوله: وَنُظِرَ فِيهِ... إلخ) قد يقال : لا عِبْرَةَ بِالإيهام المدفوع بالقرينة، على أن الإيهام لازم على الزيادة؛ إذ العبرة في الإيهام بذات اللفظ والمفهوم من اللفظ المطاوعة لا الزيادة. The Linguistic Derivation of 'Mumtaniʿ' (Impossible)
Al-Bajuri discusses three possible derivations of 'مستحيل' and argues for the redundant interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Arabic term 'مستحيل' is linguistically derived and why some interpretations are rejected in favor of others, clarifying that impossibility is inherent.

Core Idea
The derivation of 'مستحيل' shows that impossibility is inherent, not acquired.

Al-Bajuri examines the Arabic roots of the key kalam term 'مستحيل' (impossible). He considers three possible derivations, rejecting two of them on linguistic and conceptual grounds. This analysis clarifies that the term denotes an inherent impossibility, not one that arises through external action or change. The discussion highlights the precision with which theologians used language to define theological terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noting the key terms. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to words like مطاوع, صيرورة, زائدتان.
2
Identify the three derivations
The passage discusses three possible meanings of the prefix 'است': as an intransitive marker (مطاوع), as indicating 'becoming' (صيرورة), and as redundant (زائدتان). Identify each view in the text.
3
Understand the arguments against each
Al-Bajuri rejects the first two because they imply impossibility is accidental or acquired. Follow his reasoning: the intransitive suggests external influence; 'becoming' suggests change from possibility to impossibility.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why it matters that impossibility is inherent. This aligns with the kalam view that impossibility is a necessary attribute of certain concepts, not contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعليه يكون (مستحيل) مأخوذاً من: اسْتَحَالَ مطاوع: أحال، يقال: أَحَلْتُهُ فاستحال. كذا نقله اليُوسِيُّ عن بعض مشايخه. ثم قال: وهو الظاهر اهـ. ونظر فيه: بأن المطاوَعَةَ تُوهِمُ أن هذا وَصْفٌ طَرَاً بتأثير الغير، وليس كذلك. ولا يصح أن يكونا للصيرورة؛ لأنها تقتضي أنه لم يكن مُحَالاً ثم صار، وليس كذلك. واستظهر بعض المحققين أنهما ،زائدتان فيكون المستحيل بمعنى: المحال.
And accordingly, 'مستحيل' is taken from 'استَحَالَ', the intransitive counterpart of 'أحال'; one says 'أحَلْتُهُ فاستحال' (I made it impossible, so it became impossible). Thus al-Yusi transmitted from some of his teachers. Then he said: 'And it is the apparent [view]'. And it was considered that the intransitivity suggests that this is an accidental attribute by the influence of another, which is not the case. And it is not correct for the two [letters] to be for 'becoming' because it would require that it was not impossible and then became, which is not the case. And some verifiers considered that they are redundant, so 'مستحيل' means 'مُحَال'.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic analysis of the term 'مستحيل' (impossible). Al-Bajuri begins by stating that the word is derived from 'اسْتَحَالَ', which is the intransitive form (مطاوع) of 'أحال' (to declare or make something impossible). The pattern is: 'I made it impossible (أحلته), so it became impossible (فاستحال).' He quotes al-Yusi who attributes this view to some of his teachers and considers it apparent. However, an objection is raised: treating it as the intransitive implies that impossibility is an accidental quality that comes to something through an external agent, which is not the intended meaning (impossibility is inherent, not contingent). Next, he rejects the possibility that the letters ت and س are for 'becoming' (صيرورة), because that would mean the thing was not impossible before and then became impossible, which is not true for the impossible. Finally, he notes that some investigators consider the ت and س to be redundant (زائدتان), so that 'مستحيل' simply means the same as 'مُحَال' (impossible). This shows the care taken in defining technical terms by their precise linguistic roots.

Key Terms
مستحيل
مطاوع
صيرورة
زائدتان
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The prefix 'است' in 'مستحيل' always indicates a request or becoming.
Correction: In this technical term, the prefix is either for intransitivity or is redundant, not indicating a change or request.

Checkpoint
What does al-Bajuri reject regarding the derivation of 'مستحيل'?
A) That it is derived from 'اسْتَحَالَ'
B) That the letters ت and س indicate 'becoming' (صيرورة)
C) That the letters are redundant
D) That it is taken from 'أحال'

Key Takeaway
The term 'مستحيل' is derived from 'اسْتَحَالَ' with a redundant prefix, meaning it is simply 'impossible' without implying any prior state of possibility or external influence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is not like anything', do you fully grasp that His impossibility of resemblance is not a quality that could change? How does this affect your certainty in tawheed?
?
Have you ever assumed that a term in Islamic theology carries the same linguistic nuances as everyday language, without investigating its precise meaning? What can you do to correct that habit starting today?
?
If the word 'impossible' in your own faith statements is merely a label for something you don't understand, how will you move toward a deeper conviction that impossibility is essential to Allah's attributes?

The Three Schools on the Basis of Rulings
Al-Bajuri summarizes the three madhhabs on whether rulings are based on reason or revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions of the Ash'ari, Maturidi, and Mu'tazila schools on whether moral rulings are derived from reason or revelation.

Core Idea
The three schools differ on the role of reason: Ash'aris require revelation, Mu'tazila rely on reason, Maturidis make an exception for knowledge of God.

Al-Bajuri outlines the three major schools in Islamic theology regarding the foundation of moral rulings. This passage clarifies how each school views the relationship between reason and revelation, a key point in kalam. Understanding these positions helps in later discussions about divine attributes and obligations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three schools
Read the Arabic passage once with its translation. Note the names of the three schools mentioned: Ash'aris, Maturidis, and Mu'tazila.
The passage uses the term 'shar'an' meaning 'by revelation'.
2
Understand the Ash'ari position
The Ash'aris hold that all rulings are established by revelation, but reason is a necessary condition. Note the phrase 'but with the condition of reason'.
3
Understand the Maturidi position
The Maturidis make an exception for the obligation to know God, which they say is established by reason alone due to its clarity. Other rulings require revelation.
The author clarifies that this refers to some Maturidis; early Maturidis were like Ash'aris.
4
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila hold that all rulings are established by reason based on intrinsic good and evil; revelation only reinforces what reason already knows.
This is based on the principle of rational good and evil (tahsin wa taqbih).
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how each school's view affects their approach to ethics and theology. Which school gives reason the most or least autonomy?
This difference has implications for debates about divine justice and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: شَرْعاً) أي: بالشرع بناءً على أن جميع الأحكام ثَبَتَتْ بالشرع، لكن بشرط العقل، خلافاً للماتُرِيدِيَّةِ القائلين: بأن وجوبَ معرفة الله تعالى ثَبَتَتْ بالعقل لوضوحها بخلاف سائر الأحكام والمعتزلة القائلين بأن جميع الأحكام ثبتت بالعقل والشرعُ إنما جاءَ مُقَوّياً له.
(قوله: أَيْ: بِالشَّرْعِ) أشار بذلك إلى أنه منصوب على نزع الخافض على القول بقياسية ذلك، والمراد بالشرع - كما في حاشية الهدهدي- : بعثة الرسل.
(قوله: لِوُضُوحِهَا) أي: وضوح وجوبها، لا وضوح متعلقاتها التي هي العقائد
(قوله: خِلَافاً لِلْمَاتُرِيدِيَّةِ الْقَائِلِينَ: بِأَنَّ وُجُوبَ الْمَعْرِفَةِ... إلخ) أي: لوضوحه، لا للتحسين كما قالت المعتزلة والمراد بعض الماتريدية؛ إذ المتقدمون منهم من علماء ما وراء النهر كالأشاعرة كما في شرح مُنْقِذَةِ العبيد للعلامة الجَوْهَرِيّ
فتحصل أن المذاهب ثلاثة الأول: مذهب الأشاعرة: وهو أن الأحكام كلها تثبت بالشرع لكن بشرط العقل والثاني مذهب الماتريدية: وهو التفصيل بين وجوب المعرفة وبين سائر الأحكام. والثالثُ مذهبُ المعتزلة: وهو أن الأحكام كلها ثبتت بالعقل بناءً على التحسين والتقبيح العقليين. فتأمل.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: بِنَاءً عَلَى التَّحْسِينِ... إلخ) فما أدرك العقل مفسدةً في فعله كالظلم فهو الحرام، وما أدرك مفسدة في تركه فهو الواجب كالعدل، وما أدرك مصلحة في فعله ولم يدرك مفسدة في تركه فهو المندوب وما أدرك مصلحة في تركه ولم يدرك مفسدة في فعله فهو المكروه، وما لم يدرك مصلحة في فعله ولا تركه ولا مفسدة فيهما فهو المباح. اهـ ملخصاً من 'حاشية الهدهدي'.
His saying: 'by the Law' meaning by revelation, based on the fact that all rulings are established by revelation, but with the condition of reason, contrary to the Maturidis who say that the obligation to know Allah is established by reason due to its clarity, unlike other rulings, and to the Mu'tazila who say that all rulings are established by reason and that revelation only came as a reinforcement. (His saying: i.e., by revelation) He indicated by that that it is in the accusative due to removing the preposition according to the view that that is a regular process, and the meaning of revelation is the mission of messengers. (His saying: due to its clarity) meaning clarity of its obligation, not clarity of its objects which are beliefs. (His saying: contrary to the Maturidis who say that the obligation of knowledge...) i.e., due to its clarity, not due to improvement as the Mu'tazila said, and what is meant is some of the Maturidis; for the early ones among them, from the scholars of Transoxiana, are like the Ash'aris as in the explanation of Munqidh al-Abid by al-Jawhari. It becomes clear that the schools are three: the first, the Ash'ari school, that all rulings are established by revelation but with the condition of reason; the second, the Maturidi school, that there is differentiation between the obligation to know God and the rest of the rulings; the third, the Mu'tazila school, that all rulings are established by reason based on rational improvement and deterioration. So reflect. Commentary of the distinguished scholar Ahmad al-Ujhuri: (His saying: 'based on improvement...') So what reason perceives as harmful to do, like injustice, is forbidden; what it perceives as harmful to avoid, like justice, is obligatory; what it perceives as beneficial to do and no harm in avoiding, is recommended; what it perceives as beneficial to avoid and no harm in doing, is disliked; and what it perceives as no benefit or harm in either, is permissible. End quote summarized from the Hashiya of al-Hadadi.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage explains the basis of rulings according to the three theological schools. The Ash'aris hold that all rulings are from revelation, but reason is a necessary condition. The Maturidis distinguish the obligation to know God as purely rational, while other rulings require revelation. The Mu'tazila hold that all rulings are rationally derived based on intrinsic good and evil. The hashya then details the Mu'tazila classification of actions into five categories (forbidden, obligatory, recommended, disliked, permissible) according to reason's perception of benefit and harm.

Key Terms
الأشاعرة
الماتريدية
المعتزلة
الشرع
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidis agree with the Mu'tazila that all rulings are rational.
Correction: The Maturidis only hold the obligation to know God as rational; other rulings require revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi school, which obligation is established by reason alone?
A) All obligations
B) Only the obligation to know God
C) None; all require revelation
D) Only ritual prayers

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris, Maturidis, and Mu'tazila hold distinct views on the role of reason in moral obligations, with the Maturidis occupying a middle ground.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral dilemma, do you instinctively rely more on your own reasoning or on what you know from revelation? Which school's approach does your instinct reflect, and what does that reveal about your relationship with divine guidance?
?
The Maturidis teach that the obligation to know God is clear to reason alone. Do you feel that deep down you already know God exists and deserves your worship? If so, what is holding you back from acting on that knowledge with full commitment?
?
The Ash'aris say reason is a condition but not the source of rulings. In what areas of your life have you allowed your own reasoning to override a clear command from revelation? How can you realign your priorities?

The Mu'tazila Classification of Actions
Al-Ujhuri details the Mu'tazila classification of actions into five categories based on rational good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five categories of actions according to the Mu'tazila based on rational perception of benefit and harm.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila classify actions into five categories based on reason's perception of benefit or harm in doing or omitting them.

The Mu'tazila school developed a detailed classification of human actions according to what reason perceives as good or evil. This passage from al-Ujhuri's hashya outlines the five categories: forbidden, obligatory, recommended, disliked, and permissible, all grounded in rational judgment. Understanding this classification helps clarify the Mu'tazila approach to ethics and law.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage with translation. Notice it lists five categories of actions based on reason's perception.
The passage begins with 'fama adraka al-'aqlu mafsadatan fi fi'lihi...'
2
Identify the five categories
Find each category: forbidden, obligatory, recommended, disliked, and permissible. Note their criteria.
Each is defined by whether reason perceives benefit or harm in doing or omitting.
3
Understand the criteria
Reason judges actions based on perceived benefit (maslaha) or harm (mafsada). The classification depends on whether doing or omitting the action brings benefit or harm.
For example, forbidden actions are those where doing causes harm; obligatory actions are those where omitting causes harm.
4
Compare with the Ash'ari and Maturidi views
Recall that Ash'aris and Maturidis do not accept rational good and evil as the basis for all rulings. How does this classification reflect the Mu'tazila commitment to rational ethics?
This is a direct application of the principle of tahsin wa taqbih.

Source Text

(قوله: لِوُضُوحِهَا) أي: وضوح وجوبها، لا وضوح متعلقاتها التي هي العقائد
(قوله: خِلَافاً لِلْمَاتُرِيدِيَّةِ الْقَائِلِينَ: بِأَنَّ وُجُوبَ الْمَعْرِفَةِ... إلخ) أي: لوضوحه، لا للتحسين كما قالت المعتزلة والمراد بعض الماتريدية؛ إذ المتقدمون منهم من علماء ما وراء النهر كالأشاعرة كما في شرح مُنْقِذَةِ العبيد للعلامة الجَوْهَرِيّ

?

?

?

The Fourfold Classification of Things
Defines the four categories of things with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of things according to the theory of ahwal: existents, non-existents, states, and conceptual things.

Core Idea
Things are not just existent or non-existent; there are intermediary states and conceptual things.

In this lesson, we begin with the foundational classification of all things into four categories. This division is essential for later discussions on divine attributes. We'll look at the definitions and the key distinctions between them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining the four categories
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall classification. Then read the translation to understand each term.
Focus on the definitions of existents, non-existents, states, and conceptual things.
2
Understand the definition of non-existents
Notice that non-existents include both pure non-existents (like Ibn Zayd before creation) and negative things (like God's everlastingness). The gloss clarifies this.
This shows that 'non-existent' is not a simple category.
3
Differentiate states from conceptual things
States are described as intermediary between existents and non-existents, while conceptual things have subsistence but lower than states. Try to grasp the hierarchy.
Later lessons will delve deeper into each.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبني على القول بثبوت الأحوال المبني على الطريقة القائلة بأن الأشياء أربعة أقسام موجودات وهي ما تَصِحُ رُؤْيَتُهُ، ومعدومات: ما لا تُبُوتَ له. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (قوله: وَمَعْدُومَاتٌ : وَهِيَ مَا لَا تُبُوتَ (لَهُ أي ليس له تحقق في نفسه، أو له تحقق لكن مفهومه عدميّ، فشَمِلَتِ المعدوماتُ : المعدوماتِ المَحْضَةَ، كابن زيد في حالة عدمه، والعدميات كبقاء الله ،وقدّمه وإلا وَرَدَ عليه أن الأشياء خمسةٌ بزيادة العدميات تأمل. حاشية البيجوري على السنوسية وأحوال: وهي الوَاسِطَةُ بين الموجودات والمعدومات، وأمور اعتبارية: وهي ما له ثبوت لكنه لم يَرْتَقِ إلى درجة الأحوال. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: وَهِيَ الْوَاسِطَةُ... إلخ) بأن لم تفسر بالعدم لتخرج المعدومات، ولم تمكن رؤيتها لتخرج الصفات الوجودية. (قوله: وَهِيَ مَا لَهُ ثُبوت) أي في الواقع ونفس الأمر كقيام القدرة بذاته تعالى، فإنه ثابت في الواقع بقطع النظر عن الذهن؛ لأنه عين ثبوت القدرة للذات، وهو حاصل قطعاً، بدليل استدلالهم عليه بقولهم : لو انتفت القدرة عنه تعالى إلى آخر البرهان الآتي، فإن الاستدلال عليه بذلك يدل على حصوله في الواقع؛ إذ لا يستدلّ إلا على الأمور الواقعية، ويدلُّ على حصول الأمر الاعتباري في الواقع ونفس الأمر بقطع النظر عن الذهني تقسيمهم له إلى قديم وحادث، ولو كان ذهنياً فقط؛ لبطل هذا التقسيم؛ لأن الذهني يكون إلا حادثاً، ووجه كون الحال أرقى منه على هذا أن الحال قار للذات - أي: وصف. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي - لا (قوله: وَهِيَ الْوَاسِطَةُ ... إلخ) أي: بأن كان له ثبوت في نفسه أرقى من ثبوت الاعتبار إلا أنه لم ينته إلى درجة الوجود، والحالُ تنقسم قسمين: نفسية ومعنوية، وعبارة المصنف في "شرح الكبرى" والقائلون بثبوت الحال كالقاضي وإمام الحرمين يَقْسِمُون الصفاتِ ثلاثةَ أقسام: نفسية ومعنوية ومعاني. ووجه الحصر : أن المتحقق؛ إما أن يتحقق باعتبار نفسه، أو باعتبار غيره الأول الموجود، والثاني الحال، وهو إما أن يكون الغيرُ الذي تحقق به ذاتاً موصوفة، أو معنى يَقُومُ بموصوف، الأول الحالُ النفسية، والثاني الحال المعنوية اهـ. قال اليوسي: ما ذُكِرَ من التقسيم إلى ثلاثة أقسام هو باعتبار الصفة الثبوتية. (قوله: وَأُمُورٌ اعْتِبَارِيَّةً وَهِيَ مَا لَهُ ثُبُوتٌ أي في نفسه على الخلاف في ذلك. (وقوله: لَكِنَّهُ لَمْ يَرْتَقِ إِلَى دَرَجَةِ الْأَحْوَالِ بأن كان ثبوته أقل من ثبوتها، ومثلوا الاعتبار بالإمكان والوجود وغير ذلك، وللحال بالكون قادراً والكون مريداً وغير ذلك.
Based on the affirmation of states (ahwal) based on the method that says things are four divisions: existents, which are what can be seen; and non-existents: what has no subsistence. The annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: His saying: 'And non-existents: they are what has no subsistence' meaning it has no realization in itself, or it has realization but its concept is negative, so the non-existents include pure non-existents like Ibn Zayd in the state of his non-existence, and negative things like the everlastingness of God, and he presented it, otherwise it would be objected that things become five with the addition of negative things, reflect. The gloss of al-Bajuri on al-Sanusiyya: And states (ahwal): they are the intermediary between existents and non-existents, and conceptual things (umur i'tibariyya): they are what have subsistence but have not risen to the degree of states. The gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His saying: 'And they are the intermediary... etc.' meaning they are not interpreted by non-existence to exclude non-existents, nor can they be seen to exclude existential attributes. His saying: 'And they are what have subsistence' meaning in reality and actual fact, like the subsistence of power in His essence, Exalted, for it is established in reality regardless of the mind because it is identical to the subsistence of power for the essence, and it is definitely actual, as evidenced by their argument: if power were negated from Him, Exalted, to the end of the proof that follows, for the argument for it indicates its occurrence in reality, since one only argues for real things. And it indicates the occurrence of the conceptual thing in reality and actual fact regardless of the mind, their division of it into pre-eternal and originated; if it were only mental, this division would be invalid because the mental is only originated. And the reason the state is higher than it is that the state is inherent in the essence – i.e., a description. The annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: No, his saying: 'And they are the intermediary... etc.' means that it has subsistence in itself higher than the subsistence of the conceptual, but it has not reached the degree of existence. And the state (hal) is divided into two types: nafsiyya (essential) and ma'nawiyya (mental). And the wording of the author in 'Sharh al-Kubra': Those who affirm the state, like al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn, divide attributes into three types: nafsiyya, ma'nawiyya, and ma'ani. The reason for the limitation is that what is realized either is realized by virtue of itself or by virtue of another; the first is existent, the second is the state, and it is either that the other by which it is realized is a described essence or a meaning subsisting in the described; the first is the essential state (hal nafsiyya), the second is the mental state (hal ma'nawiyya). End. Al-Yusi said: What was mentioned of the division into three types concerns positive attributive attributes. His saying: 'And conceptual things (umur i'tibariyya) they are what have subsistence' i.e., in itself, with disagreement on that. And his saying: 'But it has not risen to the degree of states' meaning that its subsistence is less than their subsistence. And they gave as examples of the conceptual: possibility, existence, and others, and of the state: being powerful and being willing, and others.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents the fourfold classification of things according to the theory of ahwal: existents (things that can be seen), non-existents (what have no subsistence), states (intermediary between existents and non-existents, having a subsistence that is not fully existential), and conceptual things (having subsistence but lower than states). The glosses clarify each category: non-existents include pure non-existents (like Ibn Zayd before existence) and negative things (like God's everlastingness). States are defined as having a subsistence that is not interpretable by non-existence and not visible, thus excluding non-existents and existential attributes. Conceptual things are shown to have real subsistence (e.g., the subsistence of power in God) as evidenced by arguments and their division into pre-eternal and originated. The passage then subdivides states into nafsiyya (essential) and ma'nawiyya (mental), citing authorities like al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn who divide attributes into nafsiyya, ma'nawiyya, and ma'ani. The reason for this division is that what is realized is either by itself (existent) or by another (state), and that other is either a described essence (essential state) or a meaning subsisting in the described (mental state). Examples of conceptual things are possibility and existence; examples of states are being powerful and being willing.

Key Terms
موجودات
معدومات
أحوال
أمور اعتبارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think things are only existent or non-existent.
Correction: The passage shows there are two additional categories: states and conceptual things, which are neither fully existent nor non-existent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many categories of things are there in the theory of ahwal?
A) Two: existents and non-existents.
B) Three: existents, non-existents, and states.
C) Four: existents, non-existents, states, and conceptual things.
D) Five: adding negative things.

Key Takeaway
The theory of ahwal divides all things into four categories: existents, non-existents, states, and conceptual things, each with a distinct level of subsistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your future intentions (which are non-existents) as if they are nothing, or do you recognize their conceptual reality and the accountability they bring?
?
The classification includes states that are neither existent nor non-existent. Do you ever hold yourself or others accountable for states (like a mode of character) that are real but not fully tangible?
?
This lesson teaches that even non-existents have a conceptual subsistence. How does this change the way you think about the past—like past sins that no longer exist but still have weight in your soul? Will you begin to address them with the seriousness they deserve?

The Nature of States and Conceptual Things
Defines states and conceptual things with commentary and evidence for real subsistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific nature of states (ahwal) and conceptual things (umur i'tibariyya), including the evidence for their real subsistence and the hierarchy between them.

Core Idea
States and conceptual things have real subsistence, not merely mental existence.

After learning the fourfold division, we now dive deeper into the two intermediate categories: states and conceptual things. This lesson examines their definitions, the evidence that conceptual things have real subsistence, and why states are considered higher.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on states and conceptual things
Pay attention to how states are defined as intermediary and conceptual things as having subsistence but lower. The gloss explains why states cannot be seen nor are non-existent.
Note the examples: being powerful and being willing are states; possibility and existence are conceptual things.
2
Understand the evidence for conceptual things
The commentary argues that conceptual things have real subsistence because they can be divided into pre-eternal and originated, which would be impossible if they were purely mental.
Consider the implication: if conceptual things were only mental, they could not be pre-eternal.
3
Grasp the hierarchy between states and conceptual things
States are higher because they are inherent in the essence (qar li al-dhat), whereas conceptual things have a weaker subsistence.
This hierarchy is important for later discussions on divine attributes.
